<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="History of the International Phonetic Alphabet">History of the International Phonetic Alphabet</a></div>
<p>In 1886, a group of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> language teachers, led by the French linguist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Passy" title="Paul Passy">Paul Passy</a>, formed what would come to be known from 1897 onwards as the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Association" title="International Phonetic Association">International Phonetic Association</a> (in French, <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">l'Association phonétique internationale</i></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Their original alphabet was based on a <a href="/wiki/Spelling_reform" title="Spelling reform">spelling reform</a> for English known as the <a href="/wiki/Romic_alphabet" title="Romic alphabet">Romic alphabet</a>, but in order to make it usable for other languages, the values of the symbols were allowed to vary from language to language.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> For example, the sound <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar fricative">ʃ</a>&#93;</span> (the <i>sh</i> in <i>shoe</i>) was originally represented with the letter &#x27e8;c&#x27e9; in English, but with the digraph &#x27e8;<i lang="fr" title="French language text">ch</i>&#x27e9; in French.<sup id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, in 1888, the alphabet was revised so as to be uniform across languages, thus providing the base for all future revisions.<sup id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The idea of making the IPA was first suggested by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Jespersen" title="Otto Jespersen">Otto Jespersen</a> in a letter to Paul Passy. It was developed by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_John_Ellis" title="Alexander John Ellis">Alexander John Ellis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Sweet" title="Henry Sweet">Henry Sweet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(phonetician)" title="Daniel Jones (phonetician)">Daniel Jones</a>, and Passy.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since its creation, the IPA has undergone a number of revisions. After revisions and expansions from the 1890s to the 1940s, the IPA remained primarily unchanged until the <a href="/wiki/Kiel_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Kiel Convention">Kiel Convention</a> in 1989. A minor revision took place in 1993 with the addition of four letters for <a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">mid central vowels</a><sup id="cite_ref-world_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and the removal of letters for <a href="/wiki/Implosive_consonant#Voiceless_implosives" title="Implosive consonant">voiceless implosives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pullum_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pullum-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The alphabet was last revised in May 2005 with the addition of a letter for a <a href="/wiki/Labiodental_flap" class="mw-redirect" title="Labiodental flap">labiodental flap</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Apart from the addition and removal of symbols, changes to the IPA have consisted largely of renaming symbols and categories and in modifying typefaces.<sup id="cite_ref-world_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet</a> for speech pathology were created in 1990 and officially adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Clinical_Phonetics_and_Linguistics_Association" title="International Clinical Phonetics and Linguistics Association">International Clinical Phonetics and Linguistics Association</a> in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IPA_chart_2018.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/IPA_chart_2018.pdf/page1-330px-IPA_chart_2018.pdf.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="427" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/IPA_chart_2018.pdf/page1-495px-IPA_chart_2018.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/IPA_chart_2018.pdf/page1-660px-IPA_chart_2018.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1275" data-file-height="1650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IPA_chart_2018.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The official chart of the IPA, revised in 2018</div></div></div>
<p>The general principle of the IPA is to provide one letter for each distinctive sound (<a href="/wiki/Segment_(linguistics)" title="Segment (linguistics)">speech segment</a>), although this practice is not followed if the sound itself is complex.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This means that:
</p>
<ul><li>It does not normally use <a href="/wiki/Multigraph_(orthography)" title="Multigraph (orthography)">combinations of letters</a> to represent single sounds, the way <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> does with &#x27e8;sh&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;th&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;ng&#x27e9;, or single letters to represent multiple sounds the way &#x27e8;x&#x27e9; represents <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ks/</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɡz/</span> in English.</li>
<li>There are no letters that have context-dependent sound values, as do "hard" and "soft" <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_C" title="Hard and soft C">&#x27e8;c&#x27e9;</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_G" title="Hard and soft G">&#x27e8;g&#x27e9;</a> in several European languages.</li>
<li>The IPA does not usually have separate letters for two sounds if no known language makes a distinction between them, a property known as "selectiveness".<sup id="cite_ref-world_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The alphabet is designed for transcribing sounds (phones), not <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a>, though it is used for phonemic transcription as well. A few letters that did not indicate specific sounds have been retired (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ˇ</span>&#x27e9;, once used for the 'compound' tone of Swedish and Norwegian, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ƞ</span>&#x27e9;, once used for the <a href="/wiki/Moraic" class="mw-redirect" title="Moraic">moraic</a> nasal of Japanese), though one remains: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɧ</span>&#x27e9;, used for the <a href="/wiki/Sj-sound" title="Sj-sound">sj-sound</a> of Swedish. When the IPA is used for phonemic transcription, the letter–sound correspondence can be rather loose. For example, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">c</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɟ</span>&#x27e9; are used in the IPA <i>Handbook</i> for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t͡ʃ/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d͡ʒ/</span>.
</p><p>Among the symbols of the IPA, 107 letters represent <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>, 31 <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritics</a> are used to modify these, and 19 additional signs indicate <a href="/wiki/Segment_(linguistics)" title="Segment (linguistics)">suprasegmental</a> qualities such as <a href="/wiki/Length_(phonetics)" title="Length (phonetics)">length</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">intonation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> These are organized into a chart; the chart displayed here is the
<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.internationalphoneticassociation.org/content/ipa-chart">official chart</a> as posted at the website of the IPA.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_forms">Letter forms</span></h3>
<p>The letters chosen for the IPA are meant to harmonize with the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, most letters are either <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a>, or modifications thereof. Some letters are neither: for example, the letter denoting the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a>, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʔ</span>&#x27e9;, has the form of a dotless <a href="/wiki/Question_mark" title="Question mark">question mark</a>, and derives originally from an <a href="/wiki/Apostrophe" title="Apostrophe">apostrophe</a>.  A few letters, such as that of the voiced <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">pharyngeal fricative</a>, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʕ</span>&#x27e9;, were inspired by other writing systems (in this case, the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic</a> letter <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text"><a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">ﻉ</a></span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">ʿayn</i></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Pullum_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pullum-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite its preference for harmonizing with the Latin script, the International Phonetic Association has occasionally admitted other letters. For example, before 1989, the IPA letters for <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">click consonants</a> were &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Bilabial_click" title="Bilabial click">ʘ</a></span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Dental_click" title="Dental click">ʇ</a></span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Alveolar_click" title="Alveolar click">ʗ</a></span>&#x27e9;, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Lateral_click" title="Lateral click">ʖ</a></span>&#x27e9;, all of which were derived either from existing IPA letters, or from Latin and Greek letters. However, except for &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʘ</span>&#x27e9;, none of these letters were widely used among <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisanists</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantuists</a>, and as a result they were replaced by the more widespread symbols &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Bilabial_click" title="Bilabial click">ʘ</a></span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Dental_click" title="Dental click">ǀ</a></span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Alveolar_click" title="Alveolar click">ǃ</a></span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Palatal_click" title="Palatal click">ǂ</a></span>&#x27e9;, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Lateral_click" title="Lateral click">ǁ</a></span>&#x27e9; at the IPA Kiel Convention in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the IPA diacritics are fully <a href="/wiki/Featural_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Featural alphabet">featural</a>, there is little systemicity in the letter forms. A <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">retroflex</a> articulation is consistently indicated with a right-swinging tail, as in &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɖ ʂ ɳ</span>&#x27e9;, and <a href="/wiki/Glottalic_ingressive" class="mw-redirect" title="Glottalic ingressive">implosion</a> by a top hook, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɓ ɗ ɠ</span>&#x27e9;, but other pseudo-featural elements are due to haphazard derivation and coincidence. For example, all nasal consonants but uvular &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɴ</span>&#x27e9; are based on the form &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">n</span>&#x27e9;: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">m ɱ n ɳ ɲ ŋ</span>&#x27e9;. However, the similarity between &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">m</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">n</span>&#x27e9; is a historical accident; &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɲ</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ŋ</span>&#x27e9; are derived from <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" class="mw-redirect" title="Typographic ligature">ligatures</a> of <i>gn</i> and <i>ng,</i> and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɱ</span>&#x27e9; is an <i>ad hoc</i> imitation of &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ŋ</span>&#x27e9;.
</p><p>Some of the new letters were ordinary Latin letters turned 180 degrees, such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɐ ɔ ə ɟ ɥ ɯ ɹ ʇ ʌ ʍ ʎ</span> (turned <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a c e f h m r t v w y</span>). This was easily done in the era of <a href="/wiki/Hot_metal_typesetting" title="Hot metal typesetting">mechanical typesetting</a>, and had the advantage of not requiring the casting of special type for IPA symbols.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Capital_letters"><span id="Capital_letters">Capital letters</span></span></h4>
<p>Full capital letters are not used as IPA symbols. They are, however, often used for <a href="/wiki/Archiphoneme" class="mw-redirect" title="Archiphoneme">archiphonemes</a> and for natural classes of phonemes (that is, as wildcards). Such usage is not part of the IPA or even standardized, and may be ambiguous between authors, but it is commonly used in conjunction with the IPA. (The <a href="/wiki/ExtIPA" class="mw-redirect" title="ExtIPA">extIPA</a> chart, for example, uses wildcards in its illustrations.) Capital letters are also basic to the <a href="/wiki/Voice_Quality_Symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Voice Quality Symbols">Voice Quality Symbols</a> sometimes used in conjunction with the IPA.
</p><p>As wildcards, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">C</span>&#x27e9; for {consonant} and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">V</span>&#x27e9; for {vowel} are ubiquitous. Other common capital-letter symbols are &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">T</span>&#x27e9; for {tone/accent} (tonicity), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">N</span>&#x27e9; for {nasal}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">P</span>&#x27e9; for {plosive}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">F</span>&#x27e9; for {fricative}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">S</span>&#x27e9; for {sibilant},<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">G</span>&#x27e9; for {glide/approximant}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">L</span>&#x27e9; for {liquid}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">R</span>&#x27e9; for {rhotic} or {resonant} (sonorant), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Ʞ</span>&#x27e9; for {click}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">A, E, O, Ʉ</span>&#x27e9; for {open, front, back, close vowel} and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">B, D, J (or Ɉ), K, Q, Φ, H</span>&#x27e9; for {labial, alveolar, post-alveolar/palatal, velar, uvular, pharyngeal, glottal consonant}, respectively, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">X</span>&#x27e9; for any sound. For example, the possible syllable shapes of Mandarin can be abstracted as ranging from <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/V/</span> (an atonic vowel) to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/CGVNᵀ/</span> (a consonant-glide-vowel-nasal syllable with tone). The letters can be modified with IPA diacritics, for example &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Cʼ</span>&#x27e9; for {ejective}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Ƈ</span>&#x27e9; for {implosive}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">N͡C</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ᴺC</span>&#x27e9; for {prenasalized consonant}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Ṽ</span>&#x27e9; for {<a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">nasal vowel</a>}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">S̬</span>&#x27e9; for {voiced sibilant}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">N̥</span>&#x27e9; for {voiceless nasal}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">P͡F</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">P<sup>F</sup></span>&#x27e9; for {affricate}, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Cʲ</span>&#x27e9; for {palatalized consonant} and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">D̪</span>&#x27e9; for {dental consonant}. In speech pathology, capital letters represent indeterminate sounds, and may be superscripted to indicate they are weakly articulated: e.g. <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ᴰ]</span> is a weak indeterminate alveolar, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ᴷ]</span> a weak indeterminate velar.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Typical examples of archiphonemic use of capital letters are &#x27e8;I&#x27e9; for the Turkish harmonic vowel set <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">{i y ɯ u</span>}<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> &#x27e8;D&#x27e9; for the conflated flapped middle consonant of American English <i>writer</i> and <i>rider</i>, and &#x27e8;N&#x27e9; for the <a href="/wiki/Homorganic" class="mw-redirect" title="Homorganic">homorganic</a> syllable-coda nasal of languages such as Spanish (essentially equivalent to the wild-card usage of the letter).
</p><p>&#x27e8;V&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;F&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;C&#x27e9; have different meanings as <a href="/wiki/Voice_Quality_Symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Voice Quality Symbols">Voice Quality Symbols</a>, where they stand for "voice" (generally meaning <a href="/wiki/Secondary_articulation" title="Secondary articulation">secondary articulation</a> rather than phonetic voicing), "falsetto" and "creak". They may take diacritics that indicate what kind of voice quality an utterance has, and may be used to extract a suprasegmental feature that occurs on all susceptible segments in a stretch of IPA. For instance, the transcription of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kʷʰuˣʷt̪ʷs̟ʷ]</span> 'cat' and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kʷʰʉˣʷt͜ʃʷ]</span> 'cats' (<a href="/wiki/Islay" title="Islay">Islay</a> dialect) can be made more economical by extracting the suprasegmental labialization of the words: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Vʷ[kʰuˣt̪s̟]</span> and  <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">Vʷ[kʰʉˣt͜ʃ]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Typography_and_iconicity">Typography and iconicity</span></h3>
<p>The International Phonetic Alphabet is based on the Latin alphabet, using as few non-Latin forms as possible.<sup id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Association created the IPA so that the sound values of most <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a> letters taken from the Latin alphabet would correspond to "international usage".<sup id="cite_ref-IPA194-196_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA194-196-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Hence, the letters &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">b</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">d</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">f</span>&#x27e9;, (<a href="/wiki/Hard_G" class="mw-redirect" title="Hard G">hard</a>) &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ</span>&#x27e9;, (non-silent) &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">h</span>&#x27e9;, (unaspirated) &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">k</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">l</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">m</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">n</span>&#x27e9;, (unaspirated) &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">p</span>&#x27e9;, (voiceless) &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">s</span>&#x27e9;, (unaspirated) &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">t</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">v</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">w</span>&#x27e9;, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">z</span>&#x27e9; have the values used in English; and the <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> letters from the Latin alphabet (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">e</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">i</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">o</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">u</span>&#x27e9;) correspond to the (long) sound values of Latin: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i]</span> is like the vowel in <i>mach<strong>i</strong>ne</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[u]</span> is as in <i>r<strong>u</strong>le</i>, etc. Other letters may differ from English, but are used with these values in other European languages, such as &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">j</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">r</span>&#x27e9;, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">y</span>&#x27e9;.
</p><p>This inventory was extended by using small-capital and cursive forms, diacritics and rotation. There are also several symbols derived or taken from the Greek alphabet, though the sound values may differ. For example, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʋ</span>&#x27e9; is a vowel in Greek, but an only indirectly related consonant in the IPA.  For most of these, subtly different <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyph</a> shapes have been devised for the IPA, namely &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɑ</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ꞵ</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɣ</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɛ</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɸ</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ꭓ</span>&#x27e9;, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʋ</span>&#x27e9;, which are encoded in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> separately from their parent Greek letters, though one of them – &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">θ</span>&#x27e9; – is not, while Greek &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">β</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">χ</span>&#x27e9; are generally used for Latin &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ꞵ</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ꭓ</span>&#x27e9;.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sound values of modified Latin letters can often be derived from those of the original letters.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> For example, letters with a rightward-facing hook at the bottom represent <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">retroflex consonants</a>; and small capital letters usually represent <a href="/wiki/Uvular_consonant" title="Uvular consonant">uvular consonants</a>. Apart from the fact that certain kinds of modification to the shape of a letter generally correspond to certain kinds of modification to the sound represented, there is no way to deduce the sound represented by a symbol from its shape (as for example in <a href="/wiki/Visible_Speech" title="Visible Speech">Visible Speech</a>) nor even any systematic relation between signs and the sounds they represent (as in <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>).
</p><p>Beyond the letters themselves, there are a variety of secondary symbols which aid in transcription. <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">Diacritic marks</a> can be combined with IPA letters to transcribe modified <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetic</a> values or <a href="/wiki/Secondary_articulation" title="Secondary articulation">secondary articulations</a>. There are also special symbols for <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">suprasegmental features</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a> that are often employed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_transcription">Types of transcription</span></h3>
<p>There are two principal types of <a href="/wiki/Bracket" title="Bracket">brackets</a> used to set off IPA transcriptions:
</p>
<ul><li>[square brackets] are used with <a href="/wiki/Phonetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonetic">phonetic</a> notations, possibly including details of the pronunciation that may not be used for distinguishing words in the language being transcribed, but which the author nonetheless wishes to document.</li>
<li>/<a href="/wiki/Slash_(punctuation)" title="Slash (punctuation)">slashes</a>/ are used for <a href="/wiki/Phonemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic">phonemic</a> notations, which note only features that are distinctive in the language, without any extraneous detail.</li></ul>
<p>For example, while the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span> sounds of <i>pin</i> and <i>spin</i> are pronounced slightly differently in English (and this difference would be meaningful in some languages), the difference is not meaningful in English. Thus <i>phonemically</i> the words are <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pɪn/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/spɪn/</span>, with the same <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span> phoneme. However, to capture the difference between them (the <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a> of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span>), they can be transcribed phonetically as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[pʰɪn]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[spɪn]</span>.
</p><p>Other conventions are less commonly seen:
</p>
<ul><li>Double slashes //...//, pipes |...|, double pipes ||...||, or braces {...} may be used around a word to denote its underlying structure, more abstract even than that of phonemes. <div class="plainlinks selfreference" style="display:inline; font-style: italic;">See <a href="/wiki/Morphophonology" title="Morphophonology">morphophonology</a> for examples.</div></li>
<li>Double square brackets <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">⟦...⟧</span> are used for extra-precise transcription. They indicate that a letter has its cardinal IPA value. For example, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">⟦a⟧</span> is an open front vowel, rather than the perhaps slightly different value (such as open central) that "<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[a]</span>" may be used to transcribe in a particular language. Thus two vowels transcribed for easy legibility as &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e]</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛ]</span>&#x27e9; may be clarified as actually being <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">⟦e̝⟧</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">⟦e⟧</span>; &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ð]</span>&#x27e9; may be more precisely <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">⟦ð̠̞ˠ⟧</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Angle brackets are used to clarify that the letters represent the original orthography of the language, or sometimes an exact transliteration of a non-Latin script, not the IPA; or, within the IPA, that the letters themselves are indicated, not the sound values that they carry. For example, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">pin</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">spin</span>&#x27e9; would be seen for those words, which do not contain the <i>ee</i> sound <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i]</span> of the IPA letter &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">i</span>&#x27e9;. Italics are perhaps more commonly used for this purpose when full words are being written (as <i>pin, spin</i> above), but may not be sufficiently clear for individual letters and digraphs.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>{<a href="/wiki/Bracket#Curly_brackets" title="Bracket">Braces</a>} are used for <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosodic</a> notation. See <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet</a> for examples in that system.</li>
<li>(Parentheses) are used for indistinguishable utterances. They are also seen for silent articulation (mouthing), where the expected phonetic transcription is derived from lip-reading, and with periods to indicate silent pauses, for example (...).</li>
<li>Double parentheses indicate obscured or unintelligible sound, as in ⸨2 syll.⸩, two audible but unidentifiable syllables.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cursive_forms">Cursive forms</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cursive_forms_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Cursive forms of the International Phonetic Alphabet">Cursive forms of the International Phonetic Alphabet</a></div>
<p>IPA letters have <a href="/wiki/Cursive" title="Cursive">cursive</a> forms designed for use in manuscripts and when taking field notes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_g">Letter g</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LowercaseG.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/LowercaseG.svg/220px-LowercaseG.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/LowercaseG.svg/330px-LowercaseG.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/LowercaseG.svg/440px-LowercaseG.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="396" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LowercaseG.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typographic variants include a double-story and single-story <i>g</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the early stages of the alphabet, the typographic variants of <i>g</i>, opentail &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ</span>&#x27e9; (<a href="/wiki/File:Opentail_g.svg" class="image"><img alt="Opentail g.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/8px-Opentail_g.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/12px-Opentail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Opentail_g.svg/16px-Opentail_g.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="115" data-file-height="196" /></a>) and looptail &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">g</span>&#x27e9; (<a href="/wiki/File:Looptail_g.svg" class="image"><img alt="Looptail g.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/8px-Looptail_g.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="12" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/12px-Looptail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/16px-Looptail_g.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="132" data-file-height="196" /></a>), represented different values, but are now regarded as equivalents. Opentail &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ</span>&#x27e9; has always represented a <a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced velar plosive">voiced velar plosive</a>, while &#x27e8;<a href="/wiki/File:Looptail_g.svg" class="image"><img alt="Looptail g.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/8px-Looptail_g.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="12" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/12px-Looptail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/16px-Looptail_g.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="132" data-file-height="196" /></a>&#x27e9; was distinguished from &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ</span>&#x27e9; and represented a <a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">voiced velar fricative</a> from 1895 to 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ǥ</span>&#x27e9; represented the fricative, until 1931 when it was replaced again by &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɣ</span>&#x27e9;.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1948, the Council of the Association recognized &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<a href="/wiki/File:Looptail_g.svg" class="image"><img alt="Looptail g.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/8px-Looptail_g.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="12" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/12px-Looptail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/16px-Looptail_g.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="132" data-file-height="196" /></a>&#x27e9; as typographic equivalents,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and this decision was reaffirmed in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> While the 1949 <i>Principles of the International Phonetic Association</i> recommended the use of &#x27e8;<a href="/wiki/File:Looptail_g.svg" class="image"><img alt="Looptail g.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/8px-Looptail_g.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="12" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/12px-Looptail_g.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Looptail_g.svg/16px-Looptail_g.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="132" data-file-height="196" /></a>&#x27e9; for a velar plosive and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ</span>&#x27e9; for an advanced one for languages where it is preferable to distinguish the two, such as Russian,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> this practice never caught on.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The 1999 <i>Handbook of the International Phonetic Association</i>, the successor to the <i>Principles</i>, abandoned the recommendation and acknowledged both shapes as acceptable variants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199919_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199919-34">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modifying_the_IPA_chart"><span id="chart"></span>Modifying the IPA chart</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png/300px-Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="388" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png/450px-Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png/600px-Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png 2x" data-file-width="1656" data-file-height="2144" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Extended_IPA_chart_2005.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The authors of textbooks or similar publications often create revised versions of the IPA chart to express their own preferences or needs. The image displays one such version. Only the black symbols are part of the IPA; common additional symbols are in grey. Some of these are in the <a href="/wiki/ExtIPA" class="mw-redirect" title="ExtIPA">extIPA</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The International Phonetic Alphabet is occasionally modified by the Association.  After each modification, the Association provides an updated simplified presentation of the alphabet in the form of a chart. (See <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="History of the International Phonetic Alphabet">History of the IPA</a>.)  Not all aspects of the alphabet can be accommodated in a chart of the size published by the IPA.  The <a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_consonant" title="Alveolo-palatal consonant">alveolo-palatal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epiglottal_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Epiglottal consonant">epiglottal</a> consonants, for example, are not included in the consonant chart for reasons of space rather than of theory (two additional columns would be required, one between the retroflex and palatal columns and the other between the pharyngeal and glottal columns), and the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_flap" title="Lateral flap">lateral flap</a> would require an additional row for that single consonant, so they are listed instead under the catchall block of "other symbols".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  The indefinitely large number of <a href="/wiki/Chao_tone_letter" class="mw-redirect" title="Chao tone letter">tone letters</a> would make a full accounting impractical even on a larger page, and only a few examples are shown.
</p><p>The procedure for modifying the alphabet or the chart is to propose the change in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_International_Phonetic_Association" title="Journal of the International Phonetic Association">Journal of the IPA</a>.</i> (See, for example, August 2008 on an <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">open central unrounded vowel</a> and August 2011 on central approximants.)<sup id="cite_ref-cambridge1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge1-36">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Reactions to the proposal may be published in the same or subsequent issues of the Journal (as in August 2009 on the open central vowel).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> A formal proposal is then put to the Council of the IPA<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> – which is elected by the membership<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> – for further discussion and a formal vote.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Only changes to the alphabet or chart that have been approved by the Council can be considered part of the official IPA. Nonetheless, many users of the alphabet, including the leadership of the Association itself, make personal changes or additions in their own practice, either for convenience in the broad phonetic or phonemic transcription of a particular language (see "Illustrations of the IPA" for individual languages in the <i>Handbook</i>, which for example may use &#x27e8;/c/&#x27e9; as a phonemic symbol for what is phonetically realized as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[tʃ]</span>),<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> or because they object to some aspect of the official version.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">Usage</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">Phonetic transcription</a></div>
<p>Although the IPA offers over 160 symbols for transcribing speech, only a relatively small subset of these will be used to transcribe any one language. It is possible to transcribe speech with various levels of precision. A precise phonetic transcription, in which sounds are described in a great deal of detail, is known as a <i>narrow transcription</i>. A coarser transcription which ignores some of this detail is called a <i>broad transcription.</i> Both are relative terms, and both are generally enclosed in square brackets.<sup id="cite_ref-IPA_1999_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA_1999-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Broad phonetic transcriptions may restrict themselves to easily heard details, or only to details that are relevant to the discussion at hand, and may differ little if at all from phonemic transcriptions, but they make no theoretical claim that all the distinctions transcribed are necessarily meaningful in the language.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RPGA_international.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/RPGA_international.svg/200px-RPGA_international.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="84" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/RPGA_international.svg/300px-RPGA_international.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/RPGA_international.svg/400px-RPGA_international.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="645" data-file-height="270" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RPGA_international.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phonetic transcriptions of the word <i>international</i> in two <a href="/wiki/English_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="English dialect">English dialects</a></div></div></div>
<p>For example, the English word <i>little</i> may be transcribed broadly using the IPA as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ˈlɪtəl/</span>, and this broad (imprecise) transcription is a more or less accurate description of many pronunciations. A narrower transcription may focus on individual or dialectical details: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈɫɪɾɫ]</span> in <a href="/wiki/General_American" class="mw-redirect" title="General American">General American</a>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈlɪʔo]</span> in <a href="/wiki/Cockney" title="Cockney">Cockney</a>, or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈɫɪːɫ]</span> in Southern US English.
</p><p>It is customary to use simpler letters, without many diacritics, in phonemic transcriptions. The choice of IPA letters may reflect the theoretical claims of the author, or merely be a convenience for typesetting. For instance, in English, either the vowel of <i>pick</i> or the vowel of <i>peak</i> may be transcribed as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span> (for the pairs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pik, piːk/</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pɪk, pik/</span>), and neither is identical to the vowel of the French word <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">pique</i></i> which is also generally transcribed <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span>. That is, letters between slashes do not have absolute values, something true of broader phonetic approximations as well. A narrow transcription may, however, be used to distinguish them: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[pʰɪk]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[pʰiːk]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[pikʲ]</span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguists">Linguists</span></h3>
<p>Although IPA is popular for transcription by linguists, American linguists often alternate use of the IPA with <a href="/wiki/Americanist_phonetic_notation" title="Americanist phonetic notation">Americanist phonetic notation</a> or use the IPA together with some <a href="/wiki/Obsolete_and_nonstandard_symbols_in_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Obsolete and nonstandard symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet">nonstandard symbols</a>, for reasons including reducing the error rate on reading handwritten transcriptions or avoiding perceived awkwardness of IPA in some situations.  The exact practice may vary somewhat between languages and even individual researchers, so authors are generally encouraged to include a chart or other explanation of their choices.<sup id="cite_ref-thomason_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomason-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_study">Language study</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:274px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English-2nd_grade-Vereshchagina-Russian-textbook-page_39.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d7/English-2nd_grade-Vereshchagina-Russian-textbook-page_39.png" decoding="async" width="272" height="366" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="272" data-file-height="366" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English-2nd_grade-Vereshchagina-Russian-textbook-page_39.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from an English language textbook used in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. The IPA is used to teach the different pronunciations of the <a href="/wiki/Th_(digraph)" title="Th (digraph)">digraph &#x27e8;th&#x27e9;</a> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/θ/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ð/</span>) and to show the pronunciation of newly introduced words <i>polite</i>, <i>everything</i>, <i>always</i>, <i>forget</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some language study programs use the IPA to teach pronunciation. For example, in Russia (and earlier in the Soviet Union) and <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, textbooks for children<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and adults<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> for studying English and French consistently use the IPA. English teachers and textbooks in Taiwan tend to use the Kenyon and Knott system, a slight typographical variant of the IPA first used in the 1944 <i><a href="/wiki/A_Pronouncing_Dictionary_of_American_English" title="A Pronouncing Dictionary of American English">Pronouncing Dictionary of American English</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictionaries">Dictionaries</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="English">English</span></h4>
<p>Many British dictionaries, including the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Monolingual_learner%27s_dictionary" title="Monolingual learner&#39;s dictionary">learner's dictionaries</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_Advanced_Learner%27s_Dictionary" title="Oxford Advanced Learner&#39;s Dictionary">Oxford Advanced Learner's Dictionary</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Advanced_Learner%27s_Dictionary" title="Cambridge Advanced Learner&#39;s Dictionary">Cambridge Advanced Learner's Dictionary</a></i>, now use <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation_respelling_for_English#International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Pronunciation respelling for English">the International Phonetic Alphabet</a> to represent the pronunciation of words.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> However, most American (and some British) volumes use one of a variety of <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation_respelling_for_English" title="Pronunciation respelling for English">pronunciation respelling</a> systems, intended to be more comfortable for readers of English. For example, the respelling systems in many American dictionaries (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Merriam-Webster Dictionary">Merriam-Webster</a></i>) use &#x27e8;y&#x27e9; for IPA <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[j]</span> and &#x27e8;sh&#x27e9; for IPA <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʃ]</span>, reflecting common representations of those sounds in written English,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> using only letters of the English <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Roman alphabet</a> and variations of them. (In IPA, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[y]</span> represents the sound of the French &#x27e8;u&#x27e9; (as in <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">tu</i></i>), and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[sh]</span> represents the pair of sounds in <i>gra<strong>ssh</strong>opper</i>.)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_languages">Other languages</span></h4>
<p>The IPA is also not universal among dictionaries in languages other than English. Monolingual dictionaries of languages with generally <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_orthography" title="Phonemic orthography">phonemic orthographies</a> generally do not bother with indicating the pronunciation of most words, and tend to use respelling systems for words with unexpected pronunciations. Dictionaries produced in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> use the IPA rarely and sometimes use the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a> for transcription of foreign words. Monolingual Hebrew dictionaries use pronunciation respelling for words with unusual spelling; for example, the <i><a href="/wiki/Even-Shoshan_Dictionary" title="Even-Shoshan Dictionary">Even-Shoshan Dictionary</a></i> respells <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r940540609">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-size:1.15em;font-family:"SBL Hebrew","SBL BibLit","Frank Ruehl CLM","Taamey Frank CLM","Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey David CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">תָּכְנִית</span> as <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r940540609"/><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">תּוֹכְנִית</span> because this word uses <a href="/wiki/Kamatz" title="Kamatz">kamatz</a> katan. Bilingual dictionaries that translate from foreign languages into <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> usually employ the IPA, but monolingual Russian dictionaries occasionally use pronunciation respelling for foreign words; for example, <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Ozhegov" title="Sergey Ozhegov">Sergey Ozhegov</a>'s dictionary adds нэ́ in brackets for the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> word пенсне (<i><i lang="fr" title="French language text"><a href="/wiki/Pince-nez" title="Pince-nez">pince-nez</a></i></i>) to indicate that the final <a href="/wiki/%D0%95" class="mw-redirect" title="Е">е</a> does not <a href="/wiki/Iotation" title="Iotation">iotate</a> the preceding <a href="/wiki/%D0%9D" class="mw-redirect" title="Н">н</a>.
</p><p>The IPA is more common in bilingual dictionaries, but there are exceptions here too. Mass-market bilingual Czech dictionaries, for instance, tend to use the IPA only for sounds not found in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_orthographies_and_case_variants">Standard orthographies and case variants</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Case_variants_of_IPA_letters" title="Case variants of IPA letters">Case variants of IPA letters</a></div>
<p>IPA letters have been incorporated into the alphabets of various languages, notably via the <a href="/wiki/Africa_Alphabet" title="Africa Alphabet">Africa Alphabet</a> in many sub-Saharan languages such as <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akan_language" title="Akan language">Akan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gbe_languages" title="Gbe languages">Gbe languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manding_languages" title="Manding languages">Manding languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lingala_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Lingala language">Lingala</a>, etc. This has created the need for capital variants. For example, <a href="/wiki/Kabiy%C3%A9_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kabiyé language">Kabiyè</a> of northern <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a> has <a href="/wiki/African_D" title="African D">Ɖ ɖ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eng_(letter)" title="Eng (letter)">Ŋ ŋ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_gamma" title="Latin gamma">Ɣ ɣ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Open_O" title="Open O">Ɔ ɔ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_epsilon" title="Latin epsilon">Ɛ ɛ</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C6%B2" title="Ʋ">Ʋ ʋ</a>. These, and others, are supported by <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>, but appear in Latin ranges other than the <a href="/wiki/IPA_Extensions_(Unicode_block)" class="mw-redirect" title="IPA Extensions (Unicode block)">IPA extensions</a>.
</p><p>In the IPA itself, however, only lower-case letters are used. The 1949 edition of the IPA handbook indicated that an asterisk &#x27e8;*&#x27e9; may be prefixed to indicate that a word is a proper name,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> but this convention was not included in the 1999 <i>Handbook</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_singing">Classical singing</span></h3>
<p>IPA has widespread use among classical singers during preparation as they are frequently required to sing in a variety of foreign languages, in addition to being taught by vocal coach in order to perfect the diction of their students and to globally improve tone quality and tuning.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Opera <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">librettos</a> are authoritatively transcribed in IPA, such as <a href="/wiki/Nico_Castel" title="Nico Castel">Nico Castel</a>'s volumes<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and Timothy Cheek's book <i>Singing in Czech</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  Opera singers' ability to read IPA was used by the site <i>Visual Thesaurus</i>, which employed several opera singers "to make recordings for the 150,000 words and phrases in VT's lexical database&#160;... for their vocal stamina, attention to the details of enunciation, and most of all, knowledge of IPA".<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">Letters</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">International Phonetic Alphabet chart</a></div>
<p>The International Phonetic Association organizes the letters of the IPA into three categories: <a href="/wiki/Pulmonic_sounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulmonic sounds">pulmonic</a> consonants, non-pulmonic consonants, and vowels.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pulmonic consonant letters are arranged singly or in pairs of voiceless (<a href="/wiki/Tenuis_consonant" title="Tenuis consonant">tenuis</a>) and voiced sounds, with these then grouped in columns from front (labial) sounds on the left to back (glottal) sounds on the right. In official publications by the IPA, two columns are omitted to save space, with the letters listed among 'other symbols',<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and with the remaining consonants arranged in rows from full closure (occlusives: stops and nasals), to brief closure (vibrants: trills and taps), to partial closure (fricatives) and minimal closure (approximants), again with a row left out to save space. In the table below, a slightly different arrangement is made: All pulmonic consonants are included in the pulmonic-consonant table, and the vibrants and laterals are separated out so that the rows reflect the common <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">lenition</a> pathway of <i>stop → fricative → approximant,</i> as well as the fact that several letters pull double duty as both fricative and approximant; <a href="/wiki/Affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Affricate">affricates</a> may be created by joining stops and fricatives from adjacent cells. Shaded cells represent articulations that are judged to be impossible.
</p><p>Vowel letters are also grouped in pairs—of unrounded and rounded vowel sounds—with these pairs also arranged from front on the left to back on the right, and from maximal closure at top to minimal closure at bottom. No vowel letters are omitted from the chart, though in the past some of the mid central vowels were listed among the 'other symbols'.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="IPA_number">IPA number</span></h3>
<p>Each character is assigned a number, to prevent confusion between similar characters (such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɵ</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">θ</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɤ</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɣ</span>, or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʃ</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʄ</span>) in such situations as the printing of manuscripts. The categories of sounds are assigned different ranges of numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The numbers are assigned to sounds and to symbols, e.g. 304 is the <a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">open front unrounded vowel</a>, 415 is the centralization diacritic. Together, they form a symbol that represents the <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">open central unrounded vowel</a>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ä]</span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">Consonant</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pulmonic_consonants">Pulmonic consonants</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/IPA_pulmonic_consonant_chart_with_audio" title="IPA pulmonic consonant chart with audio">IPA pulmonic consonant chart with audio</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Egressive" class="mw-redirect" title="Egressive">pulmonic</a> consonant is a consonant made by obstructing the <a href="/wiki/Glottis" title="Glottis">glottis</a> (the space between the vocal cords) or <a href="/wiki/Human_mouth" title="Human mouth">oral cavity</a> (the mouth) and either simultaneously or subsequently letting out air from the lungs. Pulmonic consonants make up the majority of consonants in the IPA, as well as in human language. All consonants in the English language fall into this category.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pulmonic consonant table, which includes most consonants, is arranged in rows that designate <a href="/wiki/Manner_of_articulation" title="Manner of articulation">manner of articulation</a>, meaning how the consonant is produced, and columns that designate <a href="/wiki/Place_of_articulation" title="Place of articulation">place of articulation</a>, meaning where in the vocal tract the consonant is produced. The main chart includes only consonants with a single place of articulation.
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r942781990">.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-captioned{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;background:#f8f9fa;padding:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-caption{padding:0.2em;text-align:center;background:#f2f2ce}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-table{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-constable th{font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-constable th:not(.IPA-pulmonic-mannerarrow)[scope="col"]{text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0;padding-right:0;max-width:4em}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-constable th:not(.IPA-pulmonic-placearrow)[scope="row"]{text-align:left;vertical-align:middle}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-constable td{vertical-align:middle}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-constable:not(.IPA-common-audiotable) td:not([colspan]){text-align:center;max-width:1.2em;min-width:1.2em;padding:0.1em 0.2em;font-size:125%}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-audiotable td{padding:0}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-noleftborder{border-left:none}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-norightborder{border-right:none}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-notopborder{border-top:none}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-nobottomborder{border-bottom:none}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-shaded{background-color:#ddd}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-notes{text-align:left;font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-notes>div{float:right}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-notes>div ul{white-space:nowrap;font-weight:normal;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .IPA-common-notes>div ul li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .wraplinks .selflink{white-space:normal}</style>
</p>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">IPA help</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/IPA_pulmonic_consonant_chart_with_audio" title="IPA pulmonic consonant chart with audio"><img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/IPA_pulmonic_consonant_chart_with_audio" title="IPA pulmonic consonant chart with audio">audio</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">full chart</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Template:IPA_pulmonic_consonants" title="Template:IPA pulmonic consonants">template</a></li></ul>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Notes</b>
</p>
<ul><li>In rows where some letters appear in pairs (the <i><a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruents</a></i>), the letter to the right represents a <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced consonant</a> (except <a href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">breathy-voiced</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɦ]</span>). However, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʔ]</span> cannot be voiced, and the voicing of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʡ]</span> is ambiguous.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In the other rows (the <i><a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorants</a></i>), the single letter represents a voiced consonant.</li>
<li>Although there is a single letter for the coronal places of articulation for all consonants but fricatives, when dealing with a particular language, the letters may be treated as specifically dental, alveolar, or post-alveolar, as appropriate for that language, without diacritics.</li>
<li>Shaded areas indicate articulations judged to be impossible.</li>
<li>The letters <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʁ, ʕ, ʢ]</span> represent either voiced fricatives or approximants.</li>
<li>In many languages, such as English, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[h]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɦ]</span> are not actually glottal, fricatives, or approximants. Rather, they are bare <a href="/wiki/Phonation" title="Phonation">phonation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>It is primarily the shape of the tongue rather than its position that distinguishes the fricatives <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʃ ʒ]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɕ ʑ]</span>, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʂ ʐ]</span>.</li>
<li>Some listed phones are not known to exist as <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> in any language.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-pulmonic_consonants">Non-pulmonic consonants</span></h4>
<p>Non-pulmonic consonants are sounds whose airflow is not dependent on the lungs. These include <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">clicks</a> (found in the <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan languages</a> of Africa), <a href="/wiki/Implosive_consonant" title="Implosive consonant">implosives</a> (found in languages such as <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saraiki_language" title="Saraiki language">Saraiki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Ejective_consonant" title="Ejective consonant">ejectives</a> (found in many <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">Amerindian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Caucasus" title="Languages of the Caucasus">Caucasian languages</a>).
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r942781990"/>
</p>
