<td class="IPA-common-notes">
<div class="hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">IPA help</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">full chart</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Template:IPA_non-pulmonic_consonants" title="Template:IPA non-pulmonic consonants">template</a></li></ul>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Notes</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Clicks have traditionally been described as double articulation of a forward 'release' and a rear 'accompaniment', with the click letters representing only the release. Therefore, all clicks require two letters for proper notation: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">k͡ǂ, ɡ͡ǂ, ŋ͡ǂ, q͡ǂ, ɢ͡ǂ, ɴ͡ǂ</span>&#x27e9; <i>etc.</i>, or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ǂ͡k, ǂ͡ɡ, ǂ͡ŋ, ǂ͡q, ǂ͡ɢ, ǂ͡ɴ</span>&#x27e9;. When the dorsal articulation is omitted, a <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k]</span> may usually be assumed. However, recent research disputes the concept of 'accompaniment' and the idea that clicks are doubly articulated, with the rear occlusion instead simply being part of the airstream mechanisms.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In these approaches, the click letter represents both articulations, with the different letters representing different click 'types', there is no velar-uvular distinction, and the accompanying letter represents the manner, phonation, or airstream <a href="/wiki/Contour_(linguistics)" title="Contour (linguistics)">contour</a> of the click: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ǂ, ᶢǂ, ᵑǂ</span>&#x27e9; <i>etc.</i></li>
<li>Letters for the <a href="/wiki/Voiceless" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless">voiceless</a> implosives &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ƥ, ƭ, ƈ, ƙ, ʠ</span>&#x27e9; are no longer supported by the IPA, though they remain in Unicode. Instead, the IPA typically uses the voiced equivalent with a voiceless diacritic: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɓ̥, ʛ̥</span>&#x27e9;, <i>etc.</i>.</li>
<li>Although not confirmed as contrastive in any language, and therefore not officially recognized by the IPA, a letter for the <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_implosive" title="Voiced retroflex implosive">retroflex implosive</a>, <span title="U+1D91">&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ᶑ</span>&#x27e9;</span>, has been assigned an IPA number.</li>
<li>The ejective diacritic often stands in for a superscript glottal stop in <a href="/wiki/Glottalic_consonant" title="Glottalic consonant">glottalized</a> but pulmonic <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorants</a>, such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[mˀ]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[lˀ]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[wˀ]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[aˀ]</span>. These may also be transcribed as creaky <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[m̰]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l̰]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[w̰]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[a̰]</span>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Affricates">Affricates</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">Affricates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doubly_articulated_consonant" title="Doubly articulated consonant">co-articulated</a> stops are represented by two letters joined by a tie bar, either above or below the letters.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The six most common affricates are optionally represented by <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" class="mw-redirect" title="Typographic ligature">ligatures</a>, though this is no longer official IPA usage,<sup id="cite_ref-IPA_1999_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA_1999-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> because a great number of ligatures would be required to represent all affricates this way. Alternatively, a superscript notation for a consonant release is sometimes used to transcribe affricates, for example <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">tˢ</span> for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">t͡s</span>, paralleling <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">kˣ</span> ~ <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">k͡x</span>. The letters for the palatal plosives <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">c</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɟ</span> are often used as a convenience for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">t͡ʃ</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">d͡ʒ</span> or similar affricates, even in official IPA publications, so they must be interpreted with care.
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r942781990"/>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Co-articulated_consonants">Co-articulated consonants</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Co-articulated_consonant" title="Co-articulated consonant">Co-articulated consonants</a> are sounds that involve two simultaneous <a href="/wiki/Place_of_articulation" title="Place of articulation">places of articulation</a> (are pronounced using two parts of the <a href="/wiki/Vocal_tract" title="Vocal tract">vocal tract</a>). In English, the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[w]</span> in "went" is a coarticulated consonant, being pronounced by rounding the lips and raising the back of the tongue. Similar sounds are <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʍ]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɥ]</span>.
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r942781990"/>
</p>

<p><b>Notes</b>
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɧ]</span> is described as a "simultaneous <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʃ]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[x]</span>".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> However, this analysis is disputed. (See <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal-velar_fricative" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless palatal-velar fricative">voiceless palatal-velar fricative</a> for discussion.)</li>
<li>Multiple tie bars can be used: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a͡b͡c</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a͜b͜c</span>&#x27e9;. For instance, if a prenasalized stop is transcribed &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">m͡b</span>&#x27e9;, and a doubly articulated stop &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɡ͡b</span>&#x27e9;, then a prenasalized doubly articulated stop would be &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ŋ͡m͡ɡ͡b</span>&#x27e9;</li>
<li>On browsers that use <i><a href="/wiki/Arial_Unicode_MS" title="Arial Unicode MS">Arial Unicode MS</a></i> to display IPA characters, the following incorrectly formed sequences may look better due to a bug in that font: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">kp͡, ɡb͡, ŋm͡</span>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">Vowel</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/IPA_vowel_chart_with_audio" title="IPA vowel chart with audio">IPA vowel chart with audio</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg/200px-Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg/300px-Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg/400px-Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowel_tongue_position-front.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tongue positions of <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal vowel">cardinal</a> front vowels, with highest point indicated. The position of the highest point is used to determine vowel height and backness.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg/200px-Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg/300px-Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg/400px-Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="577" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cardinal_vowels-Jones_x-ray.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">X-ray photos</a> show the sounds <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i, u, a, ɑ]</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>The IPA defines a vowel as a sound which occurs at a syllable center.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Below is a chart depicting the vowels of the IPA. The IPA maps the vowels according to the position of the tongue.
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r942781990"/>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="" style="">

<div class="IPA-vowels-container-dot" style="-ms-transform: translate(57px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(57px, -4px); transform: translate(57px, -4px);"><img alt="•" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/5px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png" decoding="async" width="5" height="5" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/8px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/10px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></div>

</td>
<td><div class="IPA-vowels-container"><div class="IPA-vowels-container-left" style="-ms-transform: translate(13px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(13px, -4px); transform: translate(13px, -4px);"><span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a></span></span></div>
<div class="IPA-vowels-container-dot" style="-ms-transform: translate(13px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(13px, -4px); transform: translate(13px, -4px);"><img alt="•" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/5px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png" decoding="async" width="5" height="5" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/8px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/10px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></div>
<div class="IPA-vowels-container-right" style="-ms-transform: translate(13px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(13px, -4px); transform: translate(13px, -4px);"><span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Open_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open back rounded vowel">ɒ</a></span></span></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="IPA-common-notes">
<div class="hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">IPA help</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/IPA_vowel_chart_with_audio" title="IPA vowel chart with audio"><img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/IPA_vowel_chart_with_audio" title="IPA vowel chart with audio">audio</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet_chart" title="International Phonetic Alphabet chart">full chart</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Template:IPA_vowels" title="Template:IPA vowels">template</a></li></ul>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The vertical axis of the chart is mapped by <a href="/wiki/Vowel_height" class="mw-redirect" title="Vowel height">vowel height</a>. Vowels pronounced with the tongue lowered are at the bottom, and vowels pronounced with the tongue raised are at the top. For example, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɑ]</span> (the first vowel in <i>father</i>) is at the bottom because the tongue is lowered in this position. However, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i]</span> (the vowel in "meet") is at the top because the sound is said with the tongue raised to the roof of the mouth.
</p><p>In a similar fashion, the horizontal axis of the chart is determined by <a href="/wiki/Vowel_backness" class="mw-redirect" title="Vowel backness">vowel backness</a>. Vowels with the tongue moved towards the front of the mouth (such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛ]</span>, the vowel in "met") are to the left in the chart, while those in which it is moved to the back (such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʌ]</span>, the vowel in "but") are placed to the right in the chart.
</p><p>In places where vowels are paired, the right represents a <a href="/wiki/Roundedness" title="Roundedness">rounded vowel</a> (in which the lips are rounded) while the left is its unrounded counterpart.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diphthongs">Diphthongs</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">Diphthongs</a> are typically specified with a non-syllabic diacritic, as in &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">uɪ̯</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">u̯ɪ</span>&#x27e9;, or with a superscript for the on- or off-glide, as in &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">uᶦ</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ᵘɪ</span>&#x27e9;. Sometimes a tie bar is used, especially if it is difficult to tell if the diphthong is characterized by an on-glide, an off-glide or is variable: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">u͡ɪ</span>&#x27e9;.
</p><p><b>Notes</b>
</p>
<ul><li>&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a</span>&#x27e9; officially represents a front vowel, but there is little distinction between front and central open vowels, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a</span>&#x27e9; is frequently used for an open central vowel.<sup id="cite_ref-thomason_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomason-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> However, if disambiguation is required, the <a href="/wiki/Retraction_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Retraction (phonetics)">retraction diacritic</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Relative_articulation#Centralized_vowels" title="Relative articulation">centralized diacritic</a> may be added to indicate an open central vowel, as in &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a̠</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ä</span>&#x27e9;.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diacritics_and_prosodic_notation">Diacritics and prosodic notation <span id="Diacritics"></span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">Diacritics</a> are used for phonetic detail. They are added to IPA letters to indicate a modification or specification of that letter's normal pronunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-IPA15_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA15-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By being made superscript, any IPA letter may function as a diacritic, conferring elements of its articulation to the base letter. (See <a href="/wiki/Secondary_articulation" title="Secondary articulation">secondary articulation</a> for a list of superscript IPA letters supported by Unicode.) Those superscript letters listed below are specifically provided for by the IPA; others include &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">tˢ</span>&#x27e9; (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t]</span> with fricative release), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ᵗs</span>&#x27e9; (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[s]</span> with affricate onset), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ⁿd</span>&#x27e9; (prenasalized <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d]</span>), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">bʱ</span>&#x27e9; (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b]</span> with breathy voice), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">mˀ</span>&#x27e9; (glottalized <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[m]</span>), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">sᶴ</span>&#x27e9; (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[s]</span> with a flavor of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʃ]</span>), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">oᶷ</span>&#x27e9; (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[o]</span> with <a href="/wiki/Vowel_breaking" title="Vowel breaking">diphthongization</a>), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɯᵝ</span>&#x27e9; (<a href="/wiki/Compressed_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Compressed vowel">compressed</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɯ]</span>). Superscript diacritics placed after a letter are ambiguous between simultaneous modification of the sound and phonetic detail at the end of the sound. For example, labialized &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">kʷ</span>&#x27e9; may mean either simultaneous <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[w]</span> or else <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k]</span> with a labialized release. Superscript diacritics placed before a letter, on the other hand, normally indicate a modification of the onset of the sound (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">mˀ</span>&#x27e9; glottalized <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[m]</span>, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ˀm</span>&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[m]</span> with a glottal onset).
</p>

<p><b>Notes</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Aspirated"><b><a href="#ref_Aspirated">^a</a></b></span>  With aspirated voiced consonants, the aspiration is usually also voiced (voiced aspirated – but see <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_voiced" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspirated voiced">aspirated voiced</a>). Many linguists prefer one of the diacritics dedicated to breathy voice over simple aspiration, such as &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">b̤</span>&#x27e9;. Some linguists restrict this diacritic to <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorants</a>, and transcribe obstruents as &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">bʱ</span>&#x27e9;.</dd></dl>
<dl><dd><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_less_rounded_labialization"><b><a href="#ref_less_rounded_labialization">^l</a></b></span>  Since &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">xʷ</span>&#x27e9; can mean that the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[x]</span> is labialized (rounded) throughout its articulation, and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">x̜</span>&#x27e9; makes no sense (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[x]</span> is already completely unrounded), &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">x̜ʷ</span>&#x27e9; can only mean a less-labialized/rounded <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[xʷ]</span>. However, readers might mistake &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">x̜ʷ</span>&#x27e9; for "<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[x̜]</span>" with a labialized off-glide, or might wonder if the two diacritics cancel each other out. Placing the 'less rounded' diacritic under the labialization diacritic, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">xʷ̜</span>&#x27e9;, makes it clear that it is the labialization that is 'less rounded' than its cardinal IPA value.</dd></dl>
<p>Subdiacritics (diacritics normally placed below a letter) may be moved above a letter to avoid conflict with a <a href="/wiki/Descender" title="Descender">descender</a>, as in voiceless &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ŋ̊</span>&#x27e9;.<sup id="cite_ref-IPA15_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPA15-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The raising and lowering diacritics have optional forms &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">˔</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">˕</span>&#x27e9; that avoid descenders.
</p><p>The state of the <a href="/wiki/Glottis" title="Glottis">glottis</a> can be finely transcribed with diacritics. A series of alveolar plosives ranging from an open to a closed glottis <a href="/wiki/Phonation" title="Phonation">phonation</a> are:
</p>

<p>Additional diacritics are provided by the <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_IPA" class="mw-redirect" title="Extensions to the IPA">Extensions to the IPA</a> for speech pathology.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suprasegmentals">Suprasegmentals</span></h3>
<p>These symbols describe the features of a language above the level of individual consonants and vowels, such as <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosody</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Length_(phonetics)" title="Length (phonetics)">length</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a>, which often operate on syllables, words, or <a href="/wiki/Phrase" title="Phrase">phrases</a>: that is, elements such as the intensity, pitch, and gemination of the sounds of a language, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Rhythm" title="Rhythm">rhythm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">intonation</a> of speech.<sup id="cite_ref-Handbook13_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook13-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Although most of these symbols indicate distinctions that are phonemic at the word level, symbols also exist for intonation on a level <a href="/wiki/Phonological_hierarchy" title="Phonological hierarchy">greater than that of the word</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Handbook13_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook13-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Various ligatures of tone letters are used in the IPA <i>Handbook</i> despite not being found on the simplified official IPA chart.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>Finer distinctions of tone may be indicated by combining the tone diacritics and tone letters shown above, though not all IPA fonts support this. The four additional rising and falling tones supported by diacritics are high/mid rising <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷄, ɔ˧˥</span>, low rising <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷅, ɔ˩˧</span>, high falling <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷇, ɔ˥˧</span>, and low/mid falling <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷆, ɔ˧˩</span>.  That is, tone diacritics only support contour tones across three levels (high, mid, low), despite supporting five levels for register tones. For other contour tones, tone letters must be used: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ˨˦, ɔ˥˦</span>, etc.  For more complex (peaking and dipping) tones, though it is theoretically possible to combine the three tone diacritics in any permutation, in practice only generic peaking <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷈</span> and dipping <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷉</span> combinations are used. For finer detail, tone letters are again required (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ˧˥˧, ɔ˩˨˩, ɔ˦˩˧, ɔ˨˩˦</span>, etc.) The correspondence between tone diacritics and tone letters is therefore only approximate.
</p><p>A work-around for diacritics sometimes seen when a language has more than one rising or falling tone, and the author wishes to avoid the poorly legible diacritics <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ᷄, ɔ᷅, ɔ᷇, ɔ᷆</span> but does not wish to completely abandon the IPA, is to restrict generic rising <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ̌</span> and falling <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ̂</span> to the higher-pitched of the rising and falling tones, say <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ˥˧</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ˧˥</span>, and to use the old (retired) IPA subscript diacritics <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ̗</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ̖</span> for the lower-pitched rising and falling tones, say <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ˩˧</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ˧˩</span>. When a language has four or six level tones, the two mid tones are sometimes transcribed as high-mid <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ̍</span> (non-standard) and low-mid <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɔ̄</span>.
</p><p>A stress mark typically appears before the stressed syllable, and thus marks the syllable break as well as stress.  Where the syllable onset is a geminate consonant, e.g. in Italian, the consonant is commonly split by the stress mark, which means that the length sign is not used for gemination. (Thus &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">avˈvɔlse</span>&#x27e9; not *&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">aˈvvɔlse</span>&#x27e9;, *&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">aˈvːɔlse</span>&#x27e9;, or *&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">avˈːɔlse</span>&#x27e9;.) However, occasionally the stress mark is placed immediately before the stressed vowel, after any syllable onset (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">avvˈɔlse</span>&#x27e9; or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">avːˈɔlse</span>&#x27e9;).<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>  In such transcriptions, the stress mark does not function as a mark of the syllable boundary.
</p><p>Tone letters generally appear after each syllable, for a language with syllable tone (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a˧vɔ˥˩</span>&#x27e9;), or after the phonological word, for a language with <a href="/wiki/Word_tone" class="mw-redirect" title="Word tone">word tone</a> (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">avɔ˧˥˩</span>&#x27e9;). However, in older versions of the IPA, <i>ad hoc</i> tone marks were placed before the syllable, the same position as used to mark stress, and this convention is still sometimes seen (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">˧a˥˩vɔ</span>&#x27e9;, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">˧˥˩avɔ</span>&#x27e9;).
</p><p>There are three boundary markers, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">.</span>&#x27e9; for a syllable break, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">|</span>&#x27e9; for a minor prosodic break and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">‖</span>&#x27e9; for a major prosodic break. The tags 'minor' and 'major' are intentionally ambiguous. Depending on need, 'minor' may vary from a <a href="/wiki/Foot_(prosody)" title="Foot (prosody)">foot</a> break to a continuing–prosodic-unit boundary (equivalent to a comma), and while 'major' is often any intonation break, it may be restricted to a final–prosodic-unit boundary (equivalent to a period). Although not part of the IPA, the following boundary symbols are often used in conjunction with the IPA: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">μ</span>&#x27e9; for a <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)" title="Mora (linguistics)">mora</a> or mora boundary, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">σ</span>&#x27e9; for a syllable or syllable boundary, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">#</span>&#x27e9; for a word boundary, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">$</span>&#x27e9; for a phrase or intermediate boundary and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">%</span>&#x27e9; for a prosodic boundary. For example, C# is a word-final consonant, %V a post-<a href="/wiki/Pausa" title="Pausa">pausa</a> vowel, and T% an IU-final tone (edge tone).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_degree">Comparative degree</span></h3>
<p>IPA diacritics may be doubled to indicate an extra degree of the feature indicated. This is a productive process, but apart from extra-high and extra-low tones &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ə̋, ə̏</span>&#x27e9; being marked by doubled high- and low-tone diacritics, and the major <a href="/wiki/Prosodic_unit" title="Prosodic unit">prosodic break</a> &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">‖</span>&#x27e9; being marked as a double minor break &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">|</span>&#x27e9;, it is not specifically regulated by the IPA. (Note that transcription marks are similar: double slashes indicate extra (morpho)-phonemic, double square brackets especially precise, and double parentheses especially unintelligible.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>For example, the stress mark may be doubled to indicate an extra degree of stress, such as prosodic stress in English.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> An example in French, with a single stress mark for normal prosodic stress at the end of each <a href="/wiki/Prosodic_unit" title="Prosodic unit">prosodic unit</a> (marked as a minor prosodic break), and a double stress mark for contrastive/emphatic stress: <br /><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈˈɑ̃ːˈtre | məˈsjø ‖ ˈˈvwala maˈdam ‖]</span> <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">Entrez monsieur, voilà madame</i>.</i> <sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, a doubled secondary stress mark &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ˌˌ</span>&#x27e9; is commonly used for tertiary (extra-light) stress.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (August 2019)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Length_(phonetics)" title="Length (phonetics)">Length</a> is commonly extended by repeating the length mark, as in English <i>shhh!</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʃːːː]</span>, or for "overlong" segments in <a href="/wiki/Estonian_phonology" title="Estonian phonology">Estonian</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><i>vere</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/vere/</span> 'blood [gen.sg.]', <i>veere</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/veːre/</span> 'edge [gen.sg.]', <i>veere</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/veːːre/</span> 'roll [imp. 2nd sg.]'</li>
<li><i>lina</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/linɑ/</span> 'sheet', <i>linna</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/linːɑ/</span> 'town [gen. sg.]', <i>linna</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/linːːɑ/</span> 'town [ine. sg.]'</li></ul>
<p>(Normally additional degrees of length are handled by the extra-short or half-long diacritics, but in the Estonian examples, the first two cases are analyzed as simply short and long.)
</p><p>Occasionally other diacritics are doubled:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/R-colored_vowel" title="R-colored vowel">Rhoticity</a> in <a href="/wiki/Badaga_language" title="Badaga language">Badaga</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/be/</span> "mouth", <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/be˞/</span> "bangle", and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/be˞˞/</span> "crop".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Mild and strong <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">aspirations</a>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kʰ]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kʰʰ]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">Nasalization</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Palantla_Chinantec" title="Palantla Chinantec">Palantla Chinantec</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ẽ/</span> vs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e͌/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Weak vs strong <a href="/wiki/Ejective" class="mw-redirect" title="Ejective">ejectives</a>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kʼ]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kʼʼ]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Especially lowered, e.g. <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t̞̞]</span> (or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t̞˕]</span>, if the former symbol does not display properly) for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> as a weak fricative in some pronunciations of <i>register</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Especially retracted (at least on a vowel), e.g. <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ø̠̠]</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> though, depending on the font, on a consonant this could be confused with alveolar or alveolarized notation from the <a href="/wiki/ExtIPA" class="mw-redirect" title="ExtIPA">extIPA</a>, though such an issue can be easily avoided by placing the second diacritic <i>to the right of</i> the letter (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ø̠˗]</span>), rather than below the first diacritic.</li>
<li>The transcription of <a href="/wiki/Strident_vowel" title="Strident vowel">strident</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harsh_voice" title="Harsh voice">harsh voice</a> as extra-creaky <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a᷽/</span> may be motivated by the similarities of these phonations.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Obsolete_and_nonstandard_symbols">Obsolete and nonstandard symbols</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Obsolete_and_nonstandard_symbols_in_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Obsolete and nonstandard symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet">Obsolete and nonstandard symbols in the International Phonetic Alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Click_letter" title="Click letter">Click letter</a></div>
<p>The IPA once had parallel symbols from alternative proposals, but in most cases eventually settled on one for each sound. The rejected symbols are now considered obsolete. An example is the vowel letter &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɷ</span>&#x27e9;, rejected in favor of &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʊ</span>&#x27e9;. Letters for affricates and sounds with inherent secondary articulation have also been mostly rejected, with the idea that such features should be indicated with tie bars or diacritics: &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ƍ</span>&#x27e9; for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[zʷ]</span> is one. In addition, the rare voiceless implosives, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ƥ ƭ ƈ ƙ ʠ</span>&#x27e9;, have been dropped and are now usually written &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɓ̥ ɗ̥ ʄ̊ ɠ̊ ʛ̥</span>&#x27e9;. A retired set of click letters, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʇ, ʗ, ʖ</span>&#x27e9;, is still sometimes seen, as the official pipe letters &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ǀ, ǃ, ǁ</span>&#x27e9; may cause problems with legibility, especially when used with brackets ([ ] or / /), the letter &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">l</span>&#x27e9;, or the <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosodic</a> marks &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">|, ‖</span>&#x27e9; (for this reason, some publications which use the current IPA pipe letters disallow IPA brackets).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individual non-IPA letters may find their way into publications that otherwise use the standard IPA. This is especially common with:
</p>
<ul><li>Affricates, such as the Americanist <a href="/wiki/Barred_lambda" title="Barred lambda">barred lambda</a> &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ƛ</span>&#x27e9; for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t͜ɬ]</span> or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">č</span>&#x27e9; for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t͡ʃ]</span>. Some authors find the tie bars displeasing but the lack of tie bars confusing (i.e. &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">č</span>&#x27e9; for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t͡ʃ/</span> as distinct from <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/tʃ/</span>), while others simply prefer to have one letter for each segmental phoneme in a language.</li>
<li>Digits for tonal phonemes that have conventional numbers in a local tradition, such as the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese_phonology#Tones" title="Standard Chinese phonology">four tones of Standard Chinese</a>. This may be more convenient for comparison between languages and dialects than a phonetic transcription because tones often vary more than segmental phonemes do.</li>
<li>Digits for tone levels, which may improve readability and avoid confusion among similar tone values, though the lack of standardization can cause confusion (with e.g. "1" for high tone in some languages but for low tone in others).</li>
<li>Iconic extensions of standard IPA letters that can be readily understood, such as retroflex <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_implosive" title="Voiced retroflex implosive">&#x27e8;ᶑ &#x27e9;</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_retroflex_lateral_fricative" title="Voiceless retroflex lateral fricative">&#x27e8;ꞎ&#x27e9;</a>.</li></ul>
<p>In addition, there are typewriter substitutions for when IPA support is not available, such as capital &#x27e8;I, E, U, O, A&#x27e9; for <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɪ, ɛ, ʊ, ɔ, ɑ]</span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extensions">Extensions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ExtIPA_chart_(2015).pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/ExtIPA_chart_%282015%29.pdf/page1-220px-ExtIPA_chart_%282015%29.pdf.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/ExtIPA_chart_%282015%29.pdf/page1-330px-ExtIPA_chart_%282015%29.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/ExtIPA_chart_%282015%29.pdf/page1-440px-ExtIPA_chart_%282015%29.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1240" data-file-height="1753" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ExtIPA_chart_(2015).pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chart of the Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet (extIPA), as of 2015</div></div></div><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet">Extensions to the International Phonetic Alphabet</a></div>
<p>The "Extensions to the IPA", often abbreviated as "extIPA" and sometimes called "Extended IPA", are symbols whose original purpose was to accurately transcribe <a href="/wiki/Speech_disorder" title="Speech disorder">disordered speech</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/Kiel_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Kiel Convention">Kiel Convention</a> in 1989, a group of linguists drew up the initial extensions,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> which were based on the previous work of the PRDS (Phonetic Representation of Disordered Speech) Group in the early 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The extensions were first published in 1990, then modified, and published again in 1994 in the <i>Journal of the International Phonetic Association</i>, when they were officially adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Clinical_Phonetics_and_Linguistics_Association" title="International Clinical Phonetics and Linguistics Association">ICPLA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> While the original purpose was to transcribe disordered speech, linguists have used the extensions to designate a number of unique sounds within standard communication, such as hushing, gnashing teeth, and smacking lips.<sup id="cite_ref-world_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the Extensions to the IPA there are the conventions of the <a href="/wiki/Voice_Quality_Symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Voice Quality Symbols">Voice Quality Symbols</a>, which besides the concept of <a href="/wiki/Voice_quality" class="mw-redirect" title="Voice quality">voice quality</a> in phonetics include a number of symbols for additional airstream mechanisms and secondary articulations.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Segments_without_letters">Segments without letters</span></h2>
<p>The blank cells on the IPA chart can be filled without too much difficulty if the need arises. Some <i><a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc" title="Ad hoc">ad hoc</a></i> letters have appeared in the literature for the <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_lateral_flap" class="mw-redirect" title="Retroflex lateral flap">retroflex lateral flap</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_click" title="Retroflex click">retroflex clicks</a> (having the expected forms of &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɺ</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ǃ</span>&#x27e9; plus a retroflex tail; the analogous &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ᶑ</span>&#x27e9; for a <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_implosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Retroflex implosive">retroflex implosive</a> is even mentioned in the IPA <i>Handbook</i>), the voiceless lateral fricatives (now provided for by the <a href="/wiki/ExtIPA" class="mw-redirect" title="ExtIPA">extIPA</a>), the epiglottal trill (arguably covered by the generally-trilled epiglottal "fricatives" &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʜ ʢ</span>&#x27e9;), the labiodental plosives (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ȹ ȸ</span>&#x27e9; in some old Bantuist texts) and the near-close central vowels (&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ᵻ ᵿ</span>&#x27e9; in some publications). Diacritics can duplicate some of those, such as &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɭ̆</span>&#x27e9; for the lateral flap, &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">p̪ b̪</span>&#x27e9; for the labiodental plosives and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɪ̈ ʊ̈</span>&#x27e9; for the central vowels, and are able to fill in most of the remainder of the charts.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> If a sound cannot be transcribed, an asterisk &#x27e8;*&#x27e9; may be used, either as a letter or as a diacritic (as in &#x27e8;k*&#x27e9; sometimes seen for the <a href="/wiki/Korean_phonology" title="Korean phonology">Korean</a> "fortis" velar).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants_2">Consonants</span></h3>
<p>Representations of consonant sounds outside of the core set are created by adding diacritics to letters with similar sound values. The Spanish bilabial and dental approximants are commonly written as lowered fricatives, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[β̞]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ð̞]</span> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, voiced lateral fricatives would be written as raised lateral approximants, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɭ˔ ʎ̝ ʟ̝]</span>. A few languages such as <a href="/wiki/Banda_languages" title="Banda languages">Banda</a> have a bilabial flap as the preferred allophone of what is elsewhere a labiodental flap. It has been suggested that this be written with the labiodental flap letter and the advanced diacritic, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ⱱ̟]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, a labiodental trill would be written <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʙ̪]</span> (bilabial trill and the dental sign), and labiodental stops <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p̪ b̪]</span> rather than with the <i>ad hoc</i> letters sometimes found in the literature. Other taps can be written as extra-short plosives or laterals, e.g. <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɟ̆ ɢ̆ ʟ̆]</span>, though in some cases the diacritic would need to be written below the letter. A <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_trill" class="mw-redirect" title="Retroflex trill">retroflex trill</a> can be written as a retracted <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[r̠]</span>, just as non-subapical retroflex fricatives sometimes are. The remaining consonants, the uvular laterals (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʟ̠</span> <i>etc.</i>) and the palatal trill, while not strictly impossible, are very difficult to pronounce and are unlikely to occur even as allophones in the world's languages.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels_2">Vowels</span></h3>
<p>The vowels are similarly manageable by using diacritics for raising, lowering, fronting, backing, centering, and mid-centering.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> For example, the unrounded equivalent of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʊ]</span> can be transcribed as mid-centered <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɯ̽]</span>, and the rounded equivalent of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[æ]</span> as raised <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɶ̝]</span> or lowered <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[œ̞]</span> (though for those who conceive of vowel space as a triangle, simple <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɶ]</span> already is the rounded equivalent of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[æ]</span>). True mid vowels are lowered <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e̞ ø̞ ɘ̞ ɵ̞ ɤ̞ o̞]</span> or raised <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɛ̝ œ̝ ɜ̝ ɞ̝ ʌ̝ ɔ̝]</span>, while centered <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɪ̈ ʊ̈]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ä]</span> (or, less commonly, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɑ̈]</span>) are near-close and open central vowels, respectively. The only known vowels that cannot be represented in this scheme are vowels with unexpected <a href="/wiki/Roundedness" title="Roundedness">roundedness</a>, which would require a dedicated diacritic, such as protruded &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ʏʷ</span>&#x27e9; and compressed &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">uᵝ</span>&#x27e9; (or &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɪʷ</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɯᶹ</span>&#x27e9;).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbol_names">Symbol names</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naming_conventions_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Naming conventions of the International Phonetic Alphabet">Naming conventions of the International Phonetic Alphabet</a></div>
<p>An IPA symbol is often distinguished from the sound it is intended to represent, since there is not necessarily a one-to-one correspondence between letter and sound in broad transcription, making articulatory descriptions such as "mid front rounded vowel" or "voiced velar stop" unreliable. While the <i>Handbook of the International Phonetic Association</i> states that no official names exist for its symbols, it admits the presence of one or two common names for each.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The symbols also have <a href="/wiki/Nonce_word" title="Nonce word">nonce names</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> standard. In some cases, the Unicode names and the IPA names do not agree. For example, IPA calls <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ɛ</span> "epsilon", but Unicode calls it "small letter open E".
</p><p>The traditional names of the Latin and Greek letters are usually used for unmodified letters.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup> Letters which are not directly derived from these alphabets, such as <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiced pharyngeal fricative">ʕ</a>&#93;</span>, may have a variety of names, sometimes based on the appearance of the symbol or on the sound that it represents. In Unicode, some of the letters of Greek origin have Latin forms for use in IPA; the others use the letters from the Greek section.
</p><p>For diacritics, there are two methods of naming. For traditional diacritics, the IPA notes the name in a well known language; for example, <strong><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">é</span></strong> is <i><a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute</a></i>, based on the name of the diacritic in English and French. Non-traditional diacritics are often named after objects they resemble, so <strong><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">d̪</span></strong> is called <i>bridge</i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Pullum" class="mw-redirect" title="Geoffrey Pullum">Geoffrey Pullum</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=William_Ladusaw&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="William Ladusaw (page does not exist)">William Ladusaw</a> list a variety of names in use for IPA symbols, both current and retired, in addition to names of many other non-IPA phonetic symbols in their <i><a href="/wiki/Phonetic_Symbol_Guide" title="Phonetic Symbol Guide">Phonetic Symbol Guide</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pullum_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pullum-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Typefaces">Typefaces</span></h2>
<p>IPA typeface support is increasing, and is now included in several typefaces such as the <a href="/wiki/Times_New_Roman" title="Times New Roman">Times New Roman</a> versions that come with various recent computer <a href="/wiki/Operating_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a>. Diacritics are not always properly rendered, however.
</p><p>IPA typefaces that are <a href="/wiki/Free_software_Unicode_typefaces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free software Unicode typefaces">freely available</a> online include:
</p>
<ul><li>Several from the <a href="/wiki/Summer_Institute_of_Linguistics" class="mw-redirect" title="Summer Institute of Linguistics">SIL</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Charis_SIL" title="Charis SIL">Charis SIL</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doulos_SIL" title="Doulos SIL">Doulos SIL</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gentium" title="Gentium">Gentium</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dehuti&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dehuti (page does not exist)">Dehuti</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/DejaVu_fonts" title="DejaVu fonts">DejaVu Sans</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bitstream_Cyberbit" title="Bitstream Cyberbit">TITUS Cyberbit</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lato_(typeface)" title="Lato (typeface)">Lato</a> (with its derivate <a href="/wiki/Carlito_(typeface)" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlito (typeface)">Carlito</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Linux_Libertine" title="Linux Libertine">Linux Libertine</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Linux_Libertine" title="Linux Libertine">Linux Biolinum</a> (and their more recent derivates: <a href="/wiki/Linux_Libertine" title="Linux Libertine">Libertinus Serif</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Linux_Libertine" title="Linux Libertine">Libertinus Sans</a>)</li></ul>
<p>Some commercial IPA-compatible typefaces include: 
</p>
<ul><li>Brill, available from <a href="/wiki/Brill_Publishers" title="Brill Publishers">Brill Publishers</a></li>
<li>Various fonts shipped with <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> products such as <a href="/wiki/Segoe" title="Segoe">Segoe UI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calibri" title="Calibri">Calibri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambria_(typeface)" title="Cambria (typeface)">Cambria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucida_Sans_Unicode" title="Lucida Sans Unicode">Lucida Sans Unicode</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arial_Unicode_MS" title="Arial Unicode MS">Arial Unicode MS</a> and the most recent versions of <a href="/wiki/Times_New_Roman" title="Times New Roman">Times New Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arial" title="Arial">Arial</a></li></ul>
<p>These all include several ranges of characters in addition to the IPA. Modern <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">Web browsers</a> generally do not need any configuration to display these symbols, provided that a typeface capable of doing so is available to the operating system.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="ASCII_and_keyboard_transliterations">ASCII and keyboard transliterations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_ASCII_encodings_of_the_International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Comparison of ASCII encodings of the International Phonetic Alphabet">Comparison of ASCII encodings of the International Phonetic Alphabet</a></div>
<p>Several systems have been developed that map the IPA symbols to <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">ASCII</a> characters. Notable systems include <a href="/wiki/SAMPA" title="SAMPA">SAMPA</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-SAMPA" title="X-SAMPA">X-SAMPA</a>. The usage of mapping systems in on-line text has to some extent been adopted in the context input methods, allowing convenient keying of IPA characters that would be otherwise unavailable on standard keyboard layouts.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_input_using_on-screen_keyboard">Computer input using on-screen keyboard</span></h2>
<p>Online IPA keyboard utilities<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> are available, and they cover the complete range of IPA symbols and diacritics. In April 2019, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Gboard" title="Gboard">Gboard</a> for <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> and <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> added an IPA keyboard to its platform.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The inverted bridge under the &#x27e8;t&#x27e9; specifies it as <a href="/wiki/Apical_consonant" title="Apical consonant">apical</a> (pronounced with the tip of the tongue), and the superscript <i>h</i> shows that it is <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">aspirated</a> (breathy). Both these qualities cause the English <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t]</span> to sound different from the French or Spanish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t]</span>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Laminal_consonant" title="Laminal consonant">laminal</a> (pronounced with the blade of the tongue) and unaspirated <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t̻]</span>. &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">t̺ʰ</span>&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">t̻</span>&#x27e9; are thus two different IPA symbols for two different, though similar, sounds.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
For instance, <a href="/wiki/Flap_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Flap consonant">flaps and taps</a> are two different kinds of <a href="/wiki/Manner_of_articulation" title="Manner of articulation">articulation</a>, but since no language has (yet) been found to make a distinction between, say, an <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_tap" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar tap">alveolar flap</a> and an alveolar tap, the IPA does not provide such sounds with dedicated letters. Instead, it provides a single letter (in this case,&#160;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɾ]</span>) for both. Strictly speaking, this makes the IPA a partially <a href="/wiki/Phonemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic">phon<i>em</i>ic</a> alphabet, not a purely <a href="/wiki/Phonetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonetic">phon<i>et</i>ic</a> one.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
There are five basic tone diacritics and five basic tone letters, both sets of which are compounded for contour tones.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
"The non-roman letters of the International Phonetic Alphabet have been designed as far as possible to harmonize well with the roman letters. The Association does not recognize makeshift letters; It recognizes only letters which have been carefully cut so as to be in harmony with the other letters." (IPA 1949)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
For example, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p]</span> is called "Lower-case P" and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[χ]</span> is "Chi." (International Phonetic Association, <i>Handbook</i>, p.&#160;171)</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"From its earliest days [...] the International Phonetic Association has aimed to provide 'a separate sign for each distinctive sound; that is, for each sound which, being used instead of another, in the same language, can change the meaning of a word'." (International Phonetic Association, <i>Handbook</i>, p.&#160;27)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Laver, <i>Principles of Phonetics</i>, pp.&#160;174–175</span>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">&#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">S</span>&#x27e9; is particularly ambiguous. It has been used for 'stop', 'fricative', 'sibilant', 'sonorant' and 'semivowel'. The illustrations given here use, as much as possible, letters that are members of the sets they stand for: [n] is a nasal, [p] a plosive, [f] a fricative, [s] a sibilant, [l] a liquid, [r] both a rhotic and a resonant, and [ʞ] a click.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Perry (2000) <i>Phonological/phonetic assessment of an English-speaking adult with <a href="/wiki/Dysarthria" title="Dysarthria">dysarthria</a></i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For other Turkic languages, &#x27e8;I&#x27e9; may be restricted to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">{ɯ i</span>} (that is, to <i>ı i</i>), &#x27e8;U&#x27e9; to <i>u ü</i>, &#x27e8;A&#x27e9; to <i>a e/ä</i>, etc.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Proper angle brackets used in transcription are the mathematical symbols ⟨...⟩ (U+27E8 and U+27E9). These may not be supported by older fonts. Chevrons ‹...› (U+2039, U+203A) are sometimes substituted, as are the less-than and greater-than signs &lt;...&gt; (U+003C, U+003E) found on ASCII keyboards.</span>

</li>

<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFLadefogedMaddieson1996" class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Peter_Ladefoged" title="Peter Ladefoged">Ladefoged, Peter</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ian_Maddieson" title="Ian Maddieson">Maddieson, Ian</a> (1996). <i>The Sounds of the World's Languages</i>. <li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sometimes the obsolete transcription &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">k&#x2bb;</span>&#x27e9; (with a turned apostrophe) vs. &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">kʰ</span>&#x27e9; is still seen.</span>

<td><div class="IPA-vowels-container"><div class="IPA-vowels-container-left" style="-ms-transform: translate(57px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(57px, -4px); transform: translate(57px, -4px);"><span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">ä</a></span></span></div>
<div class="IPA-vowels-container-dot" style="-ms-transform: translate(57px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(57px, -4px); transform: translate(57px, -4px);"><img alt="•" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/5px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png" decoding="async" width="5" height="5" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/8px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Disc_Plain_black.svg/10px-Disc_Plain_black.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></div>

</td>
<td><div class="IPA-vowels-container"><div class="IPA-vowels-container-left" style="-ms-transform: translate(13px, -4px); -webkit-transform: translate(13px, -4px); transform: translate(13px, -4px);"><span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a></span></span></div>
